This article is about the NASA Moon program. For the deity it was named after, see [Artemis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis "Artemis"). For other uses, see [Artemis (disambiguation)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_(disambiguation) "Artemis (disambiguation)").

data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-verge20190718-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>

The **Artemis program** is a [Moon exploration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploration_of_the_Moon "Exploration of the Moon") program led by the United States' [National Aeronautics and Space Administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Aeronautics_and_Space_Administration "National Aeronautics and Space Administration") (NASA), formally established in 2017 via [Space Policy Directive 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Policy_Directive_1 "Space Policy Directive 1"). It is intended to reestablish a human presence on the Moon for the first time since the [Apollo 17](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_17 "Apollo 17") mission in 1972. The program's stated long-term goal is to establish a [permanent base on the Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moonbase "Moonbase") to facilitate [human missions to Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_mission_to_Mars "Human mission to Mars").

Two principal elements of the Artemis program are derived from the now-cancelled [Constellation program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constellation_program "Constellation program"): the [Orion spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orion_(spacecraft) "Orion (spacecraft)") and the [Space Launch System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Launch_System "Space Launch System") (SLS) (as a reincarnation of [Ares V](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ares_V "Ares V")). Other elements of the program, such as the [Lunar Gateway space station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Gateway "Lunar Gateway") and the [Human Landing System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_Landing_System "Human Landing System"), are in development by [government space agencies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_government_space_agencies "List of government space agencies") and [private spaceflight](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Private_spaceflight "Private spaceflight") companies, collaborations bound by the [Artemis Accords](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_Accords "Artemis Accords") and governmental contracts.

The Space Launch System, Orion spacecraft and the Human Landing System form the main [spaceflight](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spaceflight "Spaceflight") infrastructure for Artemis, and the Lunar Gateway plays a supporting role in human habitation. Supporting infrastructures for Artemis include the [Commercial Lunar Payload Services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#Commercial_Lunar_Payload_Services), [development of ground infrastructures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploration_Ground_Systems "Exploration Ground Systems"), [Artemis Base Camp](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_Base_Camp "Artemis Base Camp") on the Moon, Moon rovers, and spacesuits. Some aspects of the program have been criticized, such as the use of a [near-rectilinear halo orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Near-rectilinear_halo_orbit "Near-rectilinear halo orbit") and the program's sustainability.

Orion's first launch on the Space Launch System was originally set in 2016, but faced numerous delays; it launched on 16 November 2022 as the [Artemis I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_I "Artemis I") mission, with robots and mannequins aboard. As of January 2025, the crewed [Artemis II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_II "Artemis II") launch is expected to take place in April 2026, the [Artemis III](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_III "Artemis III") crewed lunar landing is scheduled for mid-2027, the [Artemis IV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_IV "Artemis IV") docking with the Lunar Gateway is planned for late 2028, the [Artemis V](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_V "Artemis V") docking with the European Space Agency's [ESPRIT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_System_Providing_Refueling,_Infrastructure_and_Telecommunications "European System Providing Refueling, Infrastructure and Telecommunications"), Canada's Canadarm3, and NASA's [Lunar Terrain Vehicle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Terrain_Vehicle "Lunar Terrain Vehicle") is planned for early 2030, and the Artemis VI docking which is expected to integrate the [Crew and Science Airlock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crew_and_Science_Airlock_Module "Crew and Science Airlock Module") with the [Lunar Gateway station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gateway_space_station "Gateway space station") is planned for early 2031. After Artemis VI, NASA plans yearly landings on the Moon from then on. However, the [NASA Inspector General](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA_Inspector_General "NASA Inspector General") has called the schedules unrealistic.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Artemis_Phase_1.jpg/570px-Artemis_Phase_1.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Artemis_Phase_1.jpg)

Planned missions of Artemis program

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Spaceships.svg/260px-Spaceships.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Spaceships.svg)

Currently operational crewed spacecraft (at least orbital class)

The Artemis program is organized around a series of SLS missions. These space missions will increase in complexity and are scheduled to occur at intervals of a year or more. NASA and its partners have planned Artemis I through Artemis V missions; later Artemis missions have also been proposed. Each SLS mission centers on the launch of an SLS launch vehicle carrying an [Orion spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orion_(spacecraft) "Orion (spacecraft)"). Missions after Artemis II will depend on support missions launched by other organizations and spacecraft for support functions.

Artemis I (2022) was the successful uncrewed test of the SLS and Orion, and was the first test flight for both craft.<sup id="cite_ref-10" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>i<span>]</span></a></sup> The Artemis I mission placed Orion into a lunar orbit and then returned to Earth. The SLS Block 1 design uses the [Interim Cryogenic Propulsion Stage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interim_Cryogenic_Propulsion_Stage "Interim Cryogenic Propulsion Stage") (ICPS) second stage, which performs the trans-lunar injection burn to send Orion to lunar space. For Artemis I, Orion braked into a polar distant retrograde [lunar orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_orbit "Lunar orbit") and remained for about six days before boosting back toward Earth. The Orion capsule separated from its service module, re-entered the atmosphere for [aerobraking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aerobraking "Aerobraking"), and splashed down in the Pacific Ocean under parachutes.<sup id="cite_ref-SFN20200518_11-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-SFN20200518-11"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup>

Artemis II (2026) is planned to be the first crewed test flight of SLS and the Orion spacecraft.<sup id="cite_ref-spo-20240109_5-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-spo-20240109-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> The four crew members will perform extensive testing in Earth orbit, and Orion will then be boosted into a [free-return trajectory around the Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circumlunar_trajectory "Circumlunar trajectory"), which will return Orion to Earth for re-entry and splashdown. Launch is scheduled for no earlier than April 2026.<sup id="cite_ref-spo-20240109_5-2" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-spo-20240109-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto_12-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-auto-12"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup>

[Artemis III](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_III "Artemis III") (2027) is planned to be the first American crewed lunar landing since Apollo 17 in December 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-spo-20240109_5-3" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-spo-20240109-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> The mission depends on a support mission to place a [Starship Human Landing System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Starship_Human_Landing_System "Starship Human Landing System") (HLS) in a [near-rectilinear halo orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Near-rectilinear_halo_orbit "Near-rectilinear halo orbit") (NRHO) of the Moon prior to the launch of SLS/Orion. After Starship HLS reaches NRHO, SLS/Orion will send the Orion spacecraft with a crew of four to dock with HLS.<sup id="cite_ref-13" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>ii<span>]</span></a></sup> Two astronauts will transfer to HLS, which will descend to the lunar surface and spend about 6.5 days on the surface.<sup id="cite_ref-14" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> The astronauts will perform at least two [Extravehicular Activities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extravehicular_activity "Extravehicular activity") (EVAs) on the surface before the HLS ascends to return them to a rendezvous with Orion. Orion will return the four astronauts to Earth. Launch is scheduled for no earlier than mid-2027.<sup id="cite_ref-spo-20240109_5-4" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-spo-20240109-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-auto_12-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-auto-12"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup>

[Artemis IV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_IV "Artemis IV") (2028) is planned to be the second crewed lunar landing mission. Orion and an upgraded [Starship HLS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Starship_HLS "Starship HLS") will dock with the Lunar Gateway station in NRHO prior to the landing. A prior support mission will deliver the first two Lunar Gateway modules to NRHO. The extra power of this mission's SLS Block 1B will allow it to deliver the I-HAB Gateway module for connection to the Lunar Gateway. Launch is scheduled for no earlier than September 2028.<sup id="cite_ref-sn-20230313_15-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-sn-20230313-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sn-20221030_16-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-sn-20221030-16"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup>

[Artemis V](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_V "Artemis V") (2030) is planned to be the third crewed lunar landing, which will deliver four astronauts to the Lunar Gateway station. The mission will deliver the [European Space Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Space_Agency "European Space Agency")'s [ESPRIT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_System_Providing_Refueling,_Infrastructure_and_Telecommunications "European System Providing Refueling, Infrastructure and Telecommunications") refueling and communications module and [Canadarm3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadarm3 "Canadarm3"), a Canadian-built robotic arm system for the Gateway. Also delivered will be NASA's [Lunar Terrain Vehicle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Terrain_Vehicle "Lunar Terrain Vehicle"). Launch is scheduled for no earlier than March 2030.<sup id="cite_ref-sn-20230313_15-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-sn-20230313-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-fy25-m2m_17-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-fy25-m2m-17"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> The mission will also be the first to use [Blue Origin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_Origin "Blue Origin")'s [Blue Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_Moon_(spacecraft) "Blue Moon (spacecraft)") lander to take astronauts to the Moon's surface.

[Artemis VI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_VI "Artemis VI") (2031) is planned to be the fourth crewed lunar landing, which will integrate the [Crew and Science Airlock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crew_and_Science_Airlock_Module "Crew and Science Airlock Module") with the Gateway space station.<sup id="cite_ref-18" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> Launch is scheduled for no earlier than March 2031.<sup id="cite_ref-fy25-m2m_17-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-fy25-m2m-17"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> As of 2024, the Airlock module is under construction.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_19-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-:0-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup>

Support missions include robotic landers, delivery of Gateway modules, Gateway logistics, delivery of the HLS, and delivery of elements of the Moon base. Most of these missions are executed under NASA contracts to commercial providers.

Under the [Commercial Lunar Payload Services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercial_Lunar_Payload_Services "Commercial Lunar Payload Services") (CLPS) program, several robotic landers will deliver scientific instruments and robotic rovers to the lunar surface after Artemis I. Additional CLPS missions are planned throughout the Artemis program to deliver payloads to the Moon base. These include habitat modules and rovers in support of crewed missions.

A Human Landing System (HLS) is a spacecraft that can convey crew members from NRHO to the lunar surface, support them on the surface, and return them to NRHO. Each crewed landing needs one HLS, although some or all of the spacecraft may be reusable. Each HLS must be launched from Earth and delivered to NRHO in one or more launches. The initial commercial contract was awarded to SpaceX for two Starship HLS missions, one uncrewed and one crewed as part of Artemis III. These two missions each require one HLS launch and multiple fueling launches, all on [SpaceX Starship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Starship "SpaceX Starship") launchers. NASA later exercised an option under the initial contract to commission an upgraded Starship HLS for Artemis IV and a separate contract to [Blue Origin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_Origin "Blue Origin") to develop a third crewed lunar lander, which will make its first crewed flight as part of the Artemis V mission.

The first two Gateway modules (PPE and HALO) will be delivered to NRHO in a single launch using a [Falcon Heavy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falcon_Heavy "Falcon Heavy") launcher. Originally planned to be available prior to Artemis III, as of 2021 it is planned for availability before Artemis IV.

The Gateway will be resupplied and supported by launches of [Dragon XL](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dragon_XL "Dragon XL") spacecraft launched by Falcon Heavy. Each Dragon XL will remain attached to Gateway for up to six months. The Dragon XLs will not return to Earth, but will be disposed of, probably by deliberate crashes on the lunar surface.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Altair-orion.jpg/260px-Altair-orion.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Altair-orion.jpg)

Artist's rendering of the lunar module (left) and space capsule of the [Constellation program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constellation_program "Constellation program")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Space_Launch_System_%2816525420976%29.jpg/260px-Space_Launch_System_%2816525420976%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Space_Launch_System_(16525420976).jpg)

Artist's impression of an SLS launch

The Artemis program incorporates several major components of previously cancelled [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA") programs and missions, including the [Constellation program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constellation_program "Constellation program") and the [Asteroid Redirect Mission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asteroid_Redirect_Mission "Asteroid Redirect Mission"). Originally legislated by the [NASA Authorization Act of 2005](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA_Authorization_Act_of_2005 "NASA Authorization Act of 2005"), Constellation included the development of [Ares I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ares_I "Ares I"), [Ares V](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ares_V "Ares V"), and the Orion Crew Exploration Vehicle. The program ran from the early 2000s until 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-20" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup>

In May 2009, U.S. President [Barack Obama](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barack_Obama "Barack Obama") established the [Augustine Committee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Review_of_United_States_Human_Space_Flight_Plans_Committee "Review of United States Human Space Flight Plans Committee") to take into account several objectives including support for the [International Space Station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station "International Space Station"), development of missions beyond [low Earth orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit "Low Earth orbit") (including the Moon, Mars, and [near-Earth objects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Near-Earth_object "Near-Earth object")), and use of the commercial space industry within defined budget limits.<sup id="cite_ref-21" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> The committee concluded that the Constellation program was massively underfunded and that a 2020 Moon landing was impossible. Constellation was subsequently put on hold.<sup id="cite_ref-22" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup>

On 15 April 2010, [President Obama spoke at the Kennedy Space Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barack_Obama_space_policy_speech_at_Kennedy_Space_Center "Barack Obama space policy speech at Kennedy Space Center"), announcing the administration's plans for NASA and cancelling the non-Orion elements of Constellation on the premise that the program had become nonviable.<sup id="cite_ref-23" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> He instead proposed US$6 billion in additional funding and called for development of a new heavy-lift rocket program to be ready for construction by 2015 with crewed missions to Mars orbit by the mid-2030s.<sup id="cite_ref-24" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup>

On 11 October 2010, President Obama signed into law the [NASA Authorization Act of 2010](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA_Authorization_Act_of_2010 "NASA Authorization Act of 2010"), which included requirements for the immediate development of the Space Launch System as a follow-on launch vehicle to the [Space Shuttle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle "Space Shuttle"), and continued development of a [Crew Exploration Vehicle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crew_Exploration_Vehicle "Crew Exploration Vehicle") to be capable of supporting missions beyond low Earth orbit starting in 2016, while making use of the workforce, assets, and capabilities of the [Space Shuttle program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_program "Space Shuttle program"), Constellation program, and other NASA programs. The law also invested in space technologies and robotics capabilities tied to the overall space exploration framework, ensured continued support for [Commercial Orbital Transportation Services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercial_Orbital_Transportation_Services "Commercial Orbital Transportation Services"), [Commercial Resupply Services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercial_Resupply_Services "Commercial Resupply Services"), and expanded the [Commercial Crew Development](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercial_Crew_Development "Commercial Crew Development") program.<sup id="cite_ref-25" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup>

On 30 June 2017, President [Donald Trump](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Trump "Donald Trump") signed an executive order to re-establish the [National Space Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Space_Council "National Space Council"), chaired by Vice-President [Mike Pence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mike_Pence "Mike Pence"). The Trump administration's first budget request kept Obama-era human spaceflight programs in place: Commercial Resupply Services, Commercial Crew Development, the Space Launch System, and the Orion spacecraft for deep space missions, while reducing Earth science research and calling for the elimination of NASA's education office.<sup id="cite_ref-26" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup>

### Redefinition and naming as Artemis

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=6 "Edit section: Redefinition and naming as Artemis")\]

On 11 December 2017, president Trump signed [Space Policy Directive 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA#Space_Policy_Directive_1 "NASA"), a change in national space policy that provides for a U.S.-led, integrated program with private sector partners for a human return to the Moon, followed by missions to Mars and beyond. The policy calls for the NASA administrator to "lead an innovative and sustainable program of exploration with [commercial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Private_spaceflight "Private spaceflight") and international partners to enable human expansion across the [Solar System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System "Solar System") and to bring back to Earth new knowledge and opportunities". The effort intends to more effectively organize government, private industry, and international efforts toward returning humans to the Moon and laying the foundation of eventual human [exploration of Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploration_of_Mars "Exploration of Mars").<sup id="cite_ref-Artemis_home20190519_2-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-Artemis_home20190519-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup>

Space Policy Directive 1 authorized the lunar-focused campaign. The campaign, later named Artemis, draws upon legacy US spacecraft programs, including the [Orion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orion_(spacecraft) "Orion (spacecraft)") space capsule, the [Lunar Gateway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Gateway "Lunar Gateway") space station, and [Commercial Lunar Payload Services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercial_Lunar_Payload_Services "Commercial Lunar Payload Services"), and creates entirely new programs such as the Human Landing System. The in-development Space Launch System is expected to serve as the primary launch vehicle for Orion, while commercial launch vehicles will launch various other elements of the program.<sup id="cite_ref-Verge_May2019_27-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-Verge_May2019-27"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup>

On 26 March 2019, Vice President [Mike Pence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mike_Pence "Mike Pence") announced that NASA's Moon landing goal would be accelerated by four years with a planned landing in 2024.<sup id="cite_ref-28" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> On 14 May 2019, NASA Administrator [Jim Bridenstine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jim_Bridenstine "Jim Bridenstine") announced that the new program would be named [Artemis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis "Artemis"), after the goddess of the Moon in [Greek mythology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_mythology "Greek mythology") who is the twin sister of [Apollo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo "Apollo").<sup id="cite_ref-Verge_May2019_27-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-Verge_May2019-27"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT-2019_29-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-NYT-2019-29"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite the immediate new goals, Mars missions by the 2030s were still intended as of May 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-Artemis_home20190519_2-2" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-Artemis_home20190519-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup>

In mid-2019, NASA requested US$1.6 billion in additional funding for Artemis for fiscal year 2020,<sup id="cite_ref-Harwood_CBS_30-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-Harwood_CBS-30"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> while the Senate Appropriations Committee requested from NASA a five-year budget profile<sup id="cite_ref-31" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> which is needed for evaluation and approval by [Congress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Congress "United States Congress").<sup id="cite_ref-32" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup>

In February 2020, the [White House](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_House "White House") requested a funding increase of 12% to cover the Artemis program as part of its [fiscal year 2021 budget](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_federal_budget "United States federal budget"). The total budget would have been US$25.2 billion per year with US$3.7 billion dedicated toward a Human Landing System. NASA Chief Financial Officer [Jeff DeWit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeff_DeWit "Jeff DeWit") said he thought the agency has "a very good shot" to get this budget through Congress despite Democratic concerns around the program.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA_puts_a_price_on_a_2024_Moon_landing_—_US$35_billion_34-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-NASA_puts_a_price_on_a_2024_Moon_landing_%E2%80%94_US$35_billion-34"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> However, in July 2020 the [House Appropriations Committee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Appropriations "United States House Committee on Appropriations") rejected the White House's requested funding increase.<sup id="cite_ref-Appropriations_Committee_Releases_Fiscal_Year_2021_Commerce-Justice-Science_Funding_Bill_35-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-Appropriations_Committee_Releases_Fiscal_Year_2021_Commerce-Justice-Science_Funding_Bill-35"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> The bill proposed in the House dedicated only US$700 million toward the Human Landing System, 81% (US$3 billion) short of the requested amount.<sup id="cite_ref-36" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup>

In April 2020, NASA awarded funding to Blue Origin, Dynetics, and SpaceX for 10-month-long preliminary design studies for the HLS.<sup id="cite_ref-nsf20200501_37-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-nsf20200501-37"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ars20200430_38-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-ars20200430-38"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nasapr20200430_39-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-nasapr20200430-39"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup>

Throughout February 2021, Acting Administrator of NASA [Steve Jurczyk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steve_Jurczyk "Steve Jurczyk") reiterated those budget concerns when asked about the project's schedule,<sup id="cite_ref-40" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> clarifying that "The 2024 lunar landing goal may no longer be a realistic target \[...\]".<sup id="cite_ref-42" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup>

On 4 February 2021, the [Biden administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Presidency_of_Joe_Biden "Presidency of Joe Biden") endorsed the Artemis program.<sup id="cite_ref-sn20210204_43-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-sn20210204-43"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> More specifically, White House Press Secretary [Jen Psaki](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jen_Psaki "Jen Psaki") expressed the Biden administration's "support \[for\] this effort and endeavor".<sup id="cite_ref-44" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup>

On 16 April 2021, NASA contracted [SpaceX](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX "SpaceX") to develop, manufacture, and fly two lunar landing flights with the [Starship HLS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Starship_HLS "Starship HLS") lunar lander.<sup id="cite_ref-ars20210416_47-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-ars20210416-47"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> Blue Origin and Dynetics protested the award to the [Government Accountability Office](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_Accountability_Office "Government Accountability Office") (GAO) on 26 April.<sup id="cite_ref-sn20210426_48-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-sn20210426-48"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sn20210427_49-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-sn20210427-49"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> After the GAO rejected the protests,<sup id="cite_ref-sn20210730_50-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-sn20210730-50"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> Blue Origin sued NASA over the award,<sup id="cite_ref-verge20210816_51-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-verge20210816-51"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cnbc20210816_52-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-cnbc20210816-52"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> and NASA agreed to stop work on the contract until 1 November 2021 as the lawsuit proceeded. The judge dismissed the suit on 4 November 2021 and NASA resumed work with SpaceX.<sup id="cite_ref-53" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup>

On 25 September 2021, NASA released its first digital, interactive graphic novel in celebration of National Comic Book Day. "First Woman: NASA's Promise for Humanity" is the fictional story of Callie Rodriguez, the first woman to explore the Moon.<sup id="cite_ref-54" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup>

On 15 November 2021, an audit of [NASA's Office of Inspector General](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA_Office_of_Inspector_General "NASA Office of Inspector General") estimated the true cost of the Artemis program at about $93 billion until 2025.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA_OIG_2021_1-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-NASA_OIG_2021-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup>

In addition to the initial SpaceX contract, NASA awarded two rounds of separate contracts in May 2019<sup id="cite_ref-55" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> and September 2021,<sup id="cite_ref-56" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> on aspects of the HLS to encourage alternative designs, separately from the initial HLS development effort. It announced in March 2022 that it was developing new sustainability rules and pursuing both a Starship HLS upgrade (an option under the initial SpaceX contract) and new competing alternative designs. These came after criticism from members of Congress over lack of redundancy and competition, and led NASA to ask for additional support.<sup id="cite_ref-NASAMarch2022Update_57-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-NASAMarch2022Update-57"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WallMarch2022_58-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-WallMarch2022-58"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Artemis_I_Launch_%28NHQ202211160203%29.jpeg/220px-Artemis_I_Launch_%28NHQ202211160203%29.jpeg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Artemis_I_Launch_(NHQ202211160203).jpeg)

[Artemis I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_I "Artemis I") successfully launched from the [Kennedy Space Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center "Kennedy Space Center") on 16 November 2022.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Orion_off_the_coast_of_Baja_California.jpg/220px-Orion_off_the_coast_of_Baja_California.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Orion_off_the_coast_of_Baja_California.jpg)

[Orion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orion_(spacecraft) "Orion (spacecraft)") shortly after splashdown in the Pacific Ocean on 11 December 2022.

Artemis I was originally scheduled for late 2016, and as delays accrued, eventually for late 2021, but the launch date was then pushed back to 29 August 2022.<sup id="cite_ref-sfn-20220426_59-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-sfn-20220426-59"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Various delays related to final infrastructure repairs and weather pushed the launch date further out.<sup id="cite_ref-60" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SN-20220903_61-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-SN-20220903-61"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASA_Blogs_Sep32022_11:22_am_62-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-NASA_Blogs_Sep32022_11:22_am-62"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NSF-20220908_63-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-NSF-20220908-63"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASA_Ian_Update_0924_64-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-NASA_Ian_Update_0924-64"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASA_Ian_Update_Sept26_65-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-NASA_Ian_Update_Sept26-65"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SN-20220927_66-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-SN-20220927-66"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup>

In October 2022, NASA launch managers decided on a new launch date in November, which were again slightly delayed due to preparation and weather.<sup id="cite_ref-nasa-blog-20221012_67-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-nasa-blog-20221012-67"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNN_Storm_Nicole_Report_68-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-CNN_Storm_Nicole_Report-68"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nasa-20221108_4-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-nasa-20221108-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> On 16 November at 01:47:44 EST (06:47:44 UTC), Artemis I successfully launched from the [Kennedy Space Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center "Kennedy Space Center").<sup id="cite_ref-NAT-20221116_69-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-NAT-20221116-69"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup>

Artemis I was completed at 09:40 PST (17:40 UTC) on 11 December, when the Orion spacecraft splashed down in the Pacific Ocean, west of Baja California, after a record-breaking mission, which saw Artemis travel more than 2.3 million kilometers (1.4 million miles) on a path around the Moon before returning safely to Earth. The splashdown occurred 50 years to the day since NASA's [Apollo 17](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_17 "Apollo 17") Moon landing, the last human crewed mission to touch down on the lunar surface.<sup id="cite_ref-70" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup>

Artemis II is scheduled to launch in April 2026 as a crewed lunar flyby. The [European Service Module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Service_Module "European Service Module") for the mission was completed and handed over to NASA in 2023.<sup id="cite_ref-71" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> Testing is underway on the Orion module for Artemis II. In April 2024, Lockheed was on track to hand over the Orion module by September after testing was complete.<sup id="cite_ref-72" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> A NASA [OIG](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA_Office_of_Inspector_General "NASA Office of Inspector General") report released on May 1 reported the mission was still on track, provided corrective actions on the Orion heat shield were made.<sup id="cite_ref-76" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> The Artemis II crew planned to conduct a series of trainings and simulations prior to launch, the first of which occurred in May 2024.<sup id="cite_ref-77" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup>

The [SLS core stage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Launch_System_core_stage "Space Launch System core stage") for the mission was delivered to [Kennedy Space Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center "Kennedy Space Center") (KSC) in July 2024.<sup id="cite_ref-78" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Notably, the SLS core stage for Artemis II was the last to be fully built at the [Michoud Assembly Facility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michoud_Assembly_Facility "Michoud Assembly Facility"): future missions starting with Artemis III will have the core stage partly built after arriving at Kennedy Space Center (KSC) in Florida, managed by [Exploration Ground Systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploration_Ground_Systems "Exploration Ground Systems"), which was deemed to be more efficient by program officials.<sup id="cite_ref-80" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> In July, the Orion spacecraft was moved from the testing cell to the altitude chamber inside the [Neil Armstrong Operations and Checkout Building](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neil_Armstrong_Operations_and_Checkout_Building "Neil Armstrong Operations and Checkout Building") at KSC.<sup id="cite_ref-81" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> Rocket stacking operations began on 20 November 2024, when the left aft assembly of the booster was stacked onto the Mobile Launcher. The stacking marked a crucial step for launch in late 2025.<sup id="cite_ref-82" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup>

On December 5, 2024, NASA delayed the Artemis II mission from September 2025 to April 2026, citing damage found to the heat shield of the uncrewed Orion capsule that flew on the Artemis I mission in 2022.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_83-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-:4-83"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup>

[Glover](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Victor_J._Glover "Victor J. Glover") (left), [Koch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christina_Koch "Christina Koch") (center), and [Hansen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeremy_Hansen "Jeremy Hansen") (right) are planned to be the first person of color, woman, and non-US citizen to go beyond low Earth orbit, respectively.

Artemis II is to be crewed by four astronauts: Commander [Reid Wiseman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reid_Wiseman "Reid Wiseman"), Pilot [Victor J. Glover](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Victor_J._Glover "Victor J. Glover"), Payload Specialist [Christina Koch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christina_Koch "Christina Koch"), and Mission Specialist [Jeremy Hansen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeremy_Hansen "Jeremy Hansen").<sup id="cite_ref-:2A_84-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-:2A-84"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> [Jenni Sidey-Gibbons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jenni_Sidey-Gibbons "Jenni Sidey-Gibbons") is Hansen's backup; she will join the mission if Hansen is unable to.<sup id="cite_ref-85" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup>

Glover, Koch, and Hansen are planned to be the first [person of color](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Person_of_color "Person of color"), woman, and non-US citizen to go beyond [low Earth orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit "Low Earth orbit"), respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-:2A_84-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-:2A-84"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> Hansen and Sidey-Gibbons are [Canadian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadian "Canadian") and have been assigned by the [Canadian Space Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadian_Space_Agency "Canadian Space Agency");<sup id="cite_ref-:2A_84-2" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-:2A-84"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> a 2020 treaty between the United States and Canada led to their involvement.<sup id="cite_ref-87" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup>

Artemis III is expected to launch in mid-2027 as the first crewed landing on the Moon since Apollo 17. In February 2024, NASA completed full qualification testing of the docking systems on [Starship HLS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Starship_HLS "Starship HLS").<sup id="cite_ref-88" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> Also in February, the bulk of the manufacturing for the core stage of the SLS to be used in the mission was completed.<sup id="cite_ref-89" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> In April 2024, NASA announced the successful completion of Starship’s first internal propellant transfer demonstration. The Starship’s tanker variant ability to act as an [orbital propellant depot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_propellant_depot "Orbital propellant depot") to Starship HLS is a key capability necessary to complete the Artemis III mission. A ship-to-ship propellant transfer demonstration to further prove out the capability is expected in 2025.<sup id="cite_ref-90" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> The [European Service Module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Service_Module "European Service Module") for the mission was reported on track to be handed over to NASA in summer 2024.<sup id="cite_ref-91" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> The first integrated test for the mission, which included the next generation space suits developed by Axiom Space, and the airlock module of Starship HLS was conducted in June 2024.<sup id="cite_ref-92" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup>

On December 5, 2024, NASA delayed the Artemis III mission from September 2026 to mid-2027, citing damage found to the heat shield of the uncrewed Orion capsule that flew on the Artemis I mission in 2022.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_83-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-:4-83"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup>

#### Instruments and payloads

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=11 "Edit section: Instruments and payloads")\]

In March 2024, NASA announced the scientific instruments to be included on the mission were a compact, autonomous seismometer suite called the Lunar Environment Monitoring Station, or LEMS. LEMS will characterize the regional structure of the Moon's crust and mantle to inform the development of lunar formation and evolution models. Another instrument is Lunar Effects on Agricultural Flora, a.k.a. LEAF, which will investigate the impact of the lunar surface environment on space crops. The third instrument is the Lunar Dielectric Analyzer, or LDA, an internationally contributed payload that will measure the regolith's ability to propagate an electric field.<sup id="cite_ref-93" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup>

Artemis IV is expected to launch in September 2028. Prior to the launch of the mission, a [Falcon Heavy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falcon_Heavy "Falcon Heavy") is planned to launch the first two [Lunar Gateway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Gateway "Lunar Gateway") elements: the [Power and Propulsion Element](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Power_and_Propulsion_Element "Power and Propulsion Element") and [Habitation and Logistics Outpost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Habitation_and_Logistics_Outpost "Habitation and Logistics Outpost"), [now scheduled for 2027](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Gateway#Phase_1 "Lunar Gateway"). Artemis IV will then be responsible for launching with a crew with the [International Habitation Module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Habitation_Module "International Habitation Module") (I-Hab) and adding the module to the Gateway space station. SLS Block 1B manufacture began in March 2024.<sup id="cite_ref-94" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> The I-Hab module construction was underway as of April 2024.<sup id="cite_ref-95" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> In May 2024, it was reported NASA made significant progress towards completion of Mobile Launcher 2 (ML-2), the launch platform that will be used by the larger SLS Block 1B.<sup id="cite_ref-96" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> However, in August 2024, the NASA Inspector General estimated that the launch platform could end up costing the agency $2.5 billion, more than six times its original value and may not be ready to support a launch until 2029, making the current launch schedule unrealistic.<sup id="cite_ref-97" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup>

NASA has highlighted five key points for the mission (in chronological order):

-   The crewed Orion spacecraft and Lunar I-Hab Gateway element will launch as a co-manifested payload on an SLS Block 1B rocket to lunar orbit.
-   Orion will deliver Lunar I-Hab to Gateway, where it will dock to HALO.
-   A Human Landing System spacecraft will dock to Gateway.
-   Artemis IV crew members will use the Human Landing System to descend to and later ascend from the lunar surface.
-   Artemis IV crew will return to Earth on Orion.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA_2024_98-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-NASA_2024-98"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup>

Artemis V is expected to launch in March 2030. The mission will launch four astronauts on a Space Launch System rocket and an [Orion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orion_(spacecraft) "Orion (spacecraft)") spacecraft to the [Lunar Gateway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Gateway "Lunar Gateway") and will be the third lunar landing of the Artemis program. In addition, Artemis V will deliver two new elements to the Gateway space station.<sup id="cite_ref-99" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> After docking to the Gateway, two astronauts will board the [Blue Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_Moon_(spacecraft) "Blue Moon (spacecraft)") lunar lander and fly it to the [Lunar south pole](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_south_pole "Lunar south pole") to land near the [Lunar Terrain Vehicle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Terrain_Vehicle "Lunar Terrain Vehicle") (LTV). This will be the first lunar landing since [Apollo 17](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apollo_17 "Apollo 17") to use an unpressurized lunar rover.<sup id="cite_ref-100" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup>

NASA has highlighted five key points for the mission (in chronological order):

-   The crewed Orion spacecraft and the Lunar View element will launch as a co-manifested payload on an SLS Block 1B rocket to lunar orbit.
-   Orion will deliver Lunar View to Gateway, where it will dock to HALO.
-   A Human Landing System spacecraft will dock to Gateway.
-   Artemis V crew members will use the Human Landing System to descend to and later ascend from the lunar surface.
-   Artemis V crew will return to Earth on Orion.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA_2024_98-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-NASA_2024-98"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup>

Artemis VI is expected to launch in March 2031.<sup id="cite_ref-fy25-m2m_17-2" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-fy25-m2m-17"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> According to NASA, the primary objectives of this mission would be to integrate the [Crew and Science Airlock Module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crew_and_Science_Airlock_Module "Crew and Science Airlock Module") with Gateway and complete the fourth crewed lunar surface expedition of the Artemis missions.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA_2024_98-2" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-NASA_2024-98"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> As of 2024, the Airlock module is under construction by [Mohammed bin Rashid Space Centre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mohammed_bin_Rashid_Space_Centre "Mohammed bin Rashid Space Centre").<sup id="cite_ref-:0_19-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-:0-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup>

NASA has highlighted five key points for the mission (in chronological order):

-   The crewed Orion spacecraft and Gateway’s Crew and Science Airlock will launch as a co-manifested payload on an SLS Block 1B rocket to lunar orbit.
-   Orion will deliver the Crew and Science Airlock to Gateway, where it will dock to Lunar I-Hab.
-   A Human Landing System spacecraft will dock to Gateway.
-   Artemis VI crew members will use the Human Landing System to descend to and later ascend from the lunar surface.
-   Artemis VI crew will return to Earth on Orion.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA_2024_98-3" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-NASA_2024-98"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup>

## Supporting programs

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=15 "Edit section: Supporting programs")\]

Implementation of the Artemis program will require additional programs, projects, and commercial launchers to support the construction of the Lunar Gateway, launch resupply missions to the station, and deploy numerous robotic spacecraft and instruments to the lunar surface.<sup id="cite_ref-Burghardt_May2019_101-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-Burghardt_May2019-101"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> Several precursor robotic missions are being coordinated through the [Commercial Lunar Payload Services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercial_Lunar_Payload_Services "Commercial Lunar Payload Services") (CLPS) program, which is dedicated to scouting and characterization of [lunar resources](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_resources "Lunar resources") as well as testing principles for [in-situ resource utilization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/In-situ_resource_utilization "In-situ resource utilization") (ISRU).<sup id="cite_ref-Burghardt_May2019_101-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-Burghardt_May2019-101"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CLPS_CBS_102-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-CLPS_CBS-102"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/NASA_Selects_First_Commercial_Moon_Landing_Services_for_Artemis_Program_%2847974872533%29.jpg/400px-NASA_Selects_First_Commercial_Moon_Landing_Services_for_Artemis_Program_%2847974872533%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NASA_Selects_First_Commercial_Moon_Landing_Services_for_Artemis_Program_(47974872533).jpg)

Models of the first three commercial robotic landers selected for [CLPS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercial_Lunar_Payload_Services "Commercial Lunar Payload Services"). From left: [_Peregrine_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrobotic_Technology#Peregrine_lander "Astrobotic Technology") by [Astrobotic Technology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrobotic_Technology "Astrobotic Technology"), _[Nova-C](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nova-C "Nova-C")_ by [Intuitive Machines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intuitive_Machines "Intuitive Machines"), and _Z-01_ by [OrbitBeyond](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/OrbitBeyond "OrbitBeyond").

### Commercial Lunar Payload Services

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=16 "Edit section: Commercial Lunar Payload Services")\]

In March 2018, [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA") established the [Commercial Lunar Payload Services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercial_Lunar_Payload_Services "Commercial Lunar Payload Services") (CLPS) program with the aim of sending small robotic [landers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lander_(spacecraft) "Lander (spacecraft)") and rovers mostly to the [lunar south pole region](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_south_pole "Lunar south pole") as a precursor to and in support of crewed missions.<sup id="cite_ref-CLPS_CBS_102-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-CLPS_CBS-102"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nasaspaceflight-1_104-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-nasaspaceflight-1-104"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> The main goals include scouting of [lunar resources](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_resources "Lunar resources"), ISRU feasibility testing, and lunar science.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA_20180503_105-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-NASA_20180503-105"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> NASA is awarding commercial providers [indefinite delivery/indefinite quantity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IDIQ "IDIQ") contracts to develop and fly lunar landers with scientific payloads.<sup id="cite_ref-spacenews-2_106-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-spacenews-2-106"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> The first phase considered proposals capable of delivering at least 10 kg (22 lb) of payload by the end of 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-spacenews-2_106-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-spacenews-2-106"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> Proposals for mid-sized landers capable of delivering between 500 kg (1,100 lb) and 1,000 kg (2,200 lb) of cargo were planned to also be considered for launch beyond 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-spacenews-3_107-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-spacenews-3-107"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup>

In November 2018, NASA announced the first nine companies that were qualified to bid on the CLPS transportation service contracts (see list below).<sup id="cite_ref-CLPS_qual-20181129_108-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-CLPS_qual-20181129-108"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> On 31 May 2019, three of those were awarded lander contracts: [Astrobotic Technology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrobotic_Technology "Astrobotic Technology"), [Intuitive Machines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intuitive_Machines "Intuitive Machines"), and [OrbitBeyond](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/OrbitBeyond "OrbitBeyond").<sup id="cite_ref-upi20190521_109-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-upi20190521-109"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> On 29 July 2019, NASA announced that it had granted OrbitBeyond's request to be released from obligations under the contract citing "internal corporate challenges".<sup id="cite_ref-110" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup>

The first twelve payloads and experiments from NASA centers were announced on 21 February 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-sfi20190226_111-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-sfi20190226-111"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> On 1 July 2019, NASA announced the selection of twelve additional payloads, provided by universities and industry. Seven of these are scientific investigations while five are technology demonstrations.<sup id="cite_ref-nasa-pr20190701_112-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-nasa-pr20190701-112"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup>

The Lunar Surface Instrument and Technology Payloads (LSITP) program was soliciting payloads in 2019 that do not require significant additional development. They will include technology demonstrators to advance lunar science or the commercial development of the Moon.<sup id="cite_ref-113" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup>

In November 2019, NASA added five contractors to the group of companies who are eligible to bid to send large payloads to the surface of the Moon under the CLPS program: [Blue Origin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_Origin "Blue Origin"), [Ceres Robotics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ceres_Robotics "Ceres Robotics"), [Sierra Nevada Corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sierra_Nevada_Corporation "Sierra Nevada Corporation"), [SpaceX](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX "SpaceX"), and [Tyvak Nano-Satellite Systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tyvak_Nano-Satellite_Systems "Tyvak Nano-Satellite Systems").<sup id="cite_ref-115" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASA20191127_116-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-NASA20191127-116"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup>

In April 2020, NASA selected [Masten Space Systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Masten_Space_Systems "Masten Space Systems") for a follow-on CLPS delivery of cargo to the Moon in 2022.<sup id="cite_ref-tc20200428_117-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-tc20200428-117"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> On 23 June 2021, Masten Space Systems announced it was delayed until November 2023. Dave Masten, the founder and chief technology officer, blamed the delay on the COVID pandemic and industry-wide supply chain issues.<sup id="cite_ref-119" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup>

In February 2021, NASA selected [Firefly Aerospace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Firefly_Aerospace "Firefly Aerospace") for a CLPS launch to [Mare Crisium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mare_Crisium "Mare Crisium") in mid-2023.<sup id="cite_ref-nasa-20210204_120-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-nasa-20210204-120"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup>

<table data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><caption data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b">Commercial contractors qualified to bid for CLPS NASA-funded design work</caption><tbody data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><tr data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><th 1.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_ref-124 "Jump up")** On 29 Jul 2019, NASA accepted a request for contract termination from OrbitBeyond given its identification of internal corporate challenges<sup id="cite_ref-123" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup>
