| Ispace Europe |  ![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Flag_of_Luxembourg.svg/23px-Flag_of_Luxembourg.svg.png)[Luxembourg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luxembourg "Luxembourg") | [Luxembourg City](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luxembourg_City "Luxembourg City") | Lunar in situ resource utilization | Lunar regolith transfer<sup id="cite_ref-137" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-137"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup> |

On 5 May 2020, [Reuters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reuters "Reuters") reported that the [Trump administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_presidency_of_Donald_Trump "First presidency of Donald Trump") was drafting a new international agreement outlining the laws for [mining on the Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_resources "Lunar resources").<sup id="cite_ref-138" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-138"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> NASA Administrator [Jim Bridenstine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jim_Bridenstine "Jim Bridenstine") officially announced the Artemis Accords on 15 May 2020. It consists of a series of multilateral agreements between the governments of participating nations in the Artemis program "grounded in the [Outer Space Treaty of 1967](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outer_Space_Treaty "Outer Space Treaty")".<sup id="cite_ref-sdc20200515_139-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-sdc20200515-139"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nasa-artemis20200516_140-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-nasa-artemis20200516-140"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> The Artemis Accords have been criticized by some American researchers as "a concerted, strategic effort to redirect international space cooperation in favor of short-term U.S. commercial interests".<sup id="cite_ref-Accords_141-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-Accords-141"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup>

### Exploration Ground Systems (EGS)

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=19 "Edit section: Exploration Ground Systems (EGS)")\]

The Exploration Ground Systems (EGS) Program is one of three NASA programs based at NASA's Kennedy Space Center in Florida. EGS was established to develop and operate the systems and facilities necessary to process and launch rockets and spacecraft during assembly, transport, and launch.<sup id="cite_ref-142" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-142"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> EGS is preparing the infrastructure to support NASA's Space Launch System (SLS) rocket and its payloads, such as the [Orion spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orion_spacecraft "Orion spacecraft") for Artemis I.<sup id="cite_ref-143" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-143"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-144"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup>

### Gateway Logistics Services

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=20 "Edit section: Gateway Logistics Services")\]

The [Lunar Gateway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Gateway "Lunar Gateway") is a space station to be constructed in lunar orbit, and the Gateway Logistics Services program will provide cargo and other supplies to the station, even when crews are not present.<sup id="cite_ref-145" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-145"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup> As of 2022, only SpaceX's supply vehicle, known as [Dragon XL](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dragon_XL "Dragon XL"), is planned to supply the Gateway. Dragon XL is a version of the [Dragon spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon "SpaceX Dragon"), to be launched by the [Falcon Heavy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falcon_Heavy "Falcon Heavy"). Unlike [Dragon 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon_2 "SpaceX Dragon 2") and its predecessor, it is intended to be an expendable spacecraft.

## Supporting Earth-launch vehicles

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=21 "Edit section: Supporting Earth-launch vehicles")\]

As of the early mission concepts outlined by NASA in May 2020 and refined by the HLS contract award in July 2021, the primary Earth-launch vehicles planned to support the Artemis program will include the NASA Space Launch System for the [Orion vehicle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orion_(spacecraft) "Orion (spacecraft)"), the [Falcon Heavy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falcon_Heavy "Falcon Heavy") for various components of the [Lunar Gateway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Gateway "Lunar Gateway"),<sup id="cite_ref-ars20191217_146-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-ars20191217-146"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> and the Starship HLS configuration for the eventual delivery of the [HLS vehicle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Starship_HLS "Starship HLS"). Other standard SpaceX Starships may be used later to meet other and yet to be determined crew and/or cargo handling mission needs.<sup id="cite_ref-cnbc20200430_147-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-cnbc20200430-147"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> Additional launch vehicles will also be employed later for CLPS cargo services. The European [Ariane 6](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ariane_6 "Ariane 6") has been proposed to be part of the program in July 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-aerospatium20190724_148-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-aerospatium20190724-148"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup>

The [Power and Propulsion Element](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Power_and_Propulsion_Element "Power and Propulsion Element") (PPE) module and the [Habitation and Logistics Outpost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Gateway#Planned_modules "Lunar Gateway") (HALO) of the [Gateway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Gateway "Lunar Gateway"), which were previously planned for the [SLS Block 1B](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Launch_System "Space Launch System"),<sup id="cite_ref-149" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-149"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> will now fly together on a [Falcon Heavy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falcon_Heavy "Falcon Heavy") in 2027.<sup id="cite_ref-gao-20240731_150-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-gao-20240731-150"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nasa-20210709_151-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-nasa-20210709-151"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nasa-20210209_152-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-nasa-20210209-152"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> The Gateway will be supported and resupplied by approximately 28 commercial cargo missions launched by undetermined commercial launch vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-Resupply_GLS_153-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-Resupply_GLS-153"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup> The [Gateway Logistics Services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gateway_Logistics_Services "Gateway Logistics Services") (GLS) will be in charge of resupply missions.<sup id="cite_ref-Resupply_GLS_153-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-Resupply_GLS-153"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup> GLS has also contracted for the construction of a resupply vehicle, Dragon XL, capable of remaining docked to the Gateway for one year of operations, providing and generating its own power while docked, and capable of autonomous disposal at the end of its mission.<sup id="cite_ref-Resupply_GLS_153-2" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-Resupply_GLS-153"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-154"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-155"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup>

In May 2019, the plan was for components of a crewed lunar lander to be deployed to the Gateway on commercial launchers before the arrival of the first crewed mission, [Artemis III](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_III "Artemis III").<sup id="cite_ref-a3-1_156-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-a3-1-156"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup> An alternative approach where the HLS and Orion dock together directly was discussed.<sup id="cite_ref-157" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-157"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SFN300420_158-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-SFN300420-158"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup>

As late as mid-2019, NASA considered use of [Delta IV Heavy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Delta_IV_Heavy "Delta IV Heavy") and Falcon Heavy to launch a crewed Orion mission given SLS delays.<sup id="cite_ref-159" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-159"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> Given the complexity of conversion to a different vehicle, the agency ultimately decided to use only the SLS to launch astronauts.<sup id="cite_ref-verge20190718_9-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-verge20190718-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup>

| [Electron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rocket_Lab_Electron "Rocket Lab Electron") | [CAPSTONE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CAPSTONE_(spacecraft) "CAPSTONE (spacecraft)") | 0.3 t |  | US$7.5 million<sup id="cite_ref-bloomberg02032020_165-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-bloomberg02032020-165"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wapo10022020_166-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-wapo10022020-166"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup> | 25 May 2017<sup id="cite_ref-sfnow20170525_167-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-sfnow20170525-167"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> |
| [Starship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Starship "SpaceX Starship") | Starship HLS,  
heavy [CLPS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercial_Lunar_Payload_Services "Commercial Lunar Payload Services") payloads | 200 t<sup id="cite_ref-168" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-168"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup> | 200 t<sup id="cite_ref-169" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-169"><span>[</span>a<span>]</span></a></sup> | US$2 million (goal)<sup id="cite_ref-sdc20191106_170-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-sdc20191106-170"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-171"><span>[</span>b<span>]</span></a></sup> | 20 April 2023<sup id="cite_ref-FoustLiftOff_172-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-FoustLiftOff-172"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup> |
| [Ariane 6](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ariane_6 "Ariane 6") | [Argonaut](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argonaut_(lunar_lander) "Argonaut (lunar lander)") | 21.6 t | 8.6 t | €115 million<sup id="cite_ref-173" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-173"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lem_174-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-lem-174"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup> | 9 July 2024<sup id="cite_ref-175" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-175"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-176" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-176"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup> |
| [New Glenn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Glenn "New Glenn") | [Blue Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_Moon_(spacecraft) "Blue Moon (spacecraft)") | 45 t | 7 t | $68 million | NET 6 January 2025 |

1.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_ref-169 "Jump up")** requires refueling in LEO
2.  **[^](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_ref-171 "Jump up")** Per launch. May require refueling launches

### Space Launch System

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=22 "Edit section: Space Launch System")\]

[![Diagram of four versions of the Space Launch System rocket](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/SLS_Configuration.jpg/530px-SLS_Configuration.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:SLS_Configuration.jpg)

The planned evolution of the [Space Launch System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Launch_System "Space Launch System"), the primary launch vehicle for Orion

The [Space Launch System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Launch_System "Space Launch System") (SLS) is a United States [super heavy-lift](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Super_heavy-lift_launch_vehicle "Super heavy-lift launch vehicle") [expendable launch vehicle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expendable_launch_system "Expendable launch system"), which has been under development since its announcement in 2011. The SLS is the main Earth-launch vehicle of the Artemis lunar program, as of March 2021. NASA is required by the [U.S. Congress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Congress "United States Congress") to use SLS Block 1, which will be powerful enough to lift a [payload](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Payload "Payload") of 95 metric tons (209,000 lb) to [low Earth orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit "Low Earth orbit") (LEO), and will launch [Artemis I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_I "Artemis I"), [II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_II "Artemis II"), and [III](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_III "Artemis III").<sup id="cite_ref-177" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-177"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-178" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-178"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-179"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup> Starting in 2028, Block 1B is intended to debut the [Exploration Upper Stage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploration_Upper_Stage "Exploration Upper Stage") (EUS) and launch the notional Artemis IV–VII.<sup id="cite_ref-180" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-180"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-181"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup>

Starting in 2029, Block 2 is planned to replace the initial Shuttle-derived boosters with advanced boosters and would have a LEO capability of more than 130 metric tons (290,000 lb), again as required by Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-AuthAct10_182-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-AuthAct10-182"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup> Block 2 is intended to enable crewed launches to [Mars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mars "Mars").<sup id="cite_ref-nsf-20170406_7-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-nsf-20170406-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> The SLS will launch the [Orion spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orion_(spacecraft) "Orion (spacecraft)") and use the ground operations capabilities and launch facilities at NASA's [Kennedy Space Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center "Kennedy Space Center") in [Florida](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Florida "Florida").

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/KSC-20220318-PH-KLS01_0031.jpg/310px-KSC-20220318-PH-KLS01_0031.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:KSC-20220318-PH-KLS01_0031.jpg)

The SLS for [Artemis I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_I "Artemis I") on its mobile launcher, getting ready for a wet dress rehearsal ahead of launch

In March 2019, the [Trump administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_presidency_of_Donald_Trump "First presidency of Donald Trump") released its [Fiscal Year 2020 Budget Request](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_budget "United States budget") for NASA. This budget did not initially include any money for the Block 1B and Block 2 variants of SLS, but later a request for a budget increase of $1.6 billion towards SLS, Orion, and crewed landers was made. Block 1B is now intended to debut on Artemis IV and will be used mainly for co-manifested crew transfers and logistics rather than constructing the Gateway as initially planned. An uncrewed Block 1B was planned to launch the [Lunar Surface Asset](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_outpost_(NASA) "Lunar outpost (NASA)") in 2028, the first lunar outpost of the Artemis program, but now that launch has been moved to a commercial launcher.<sup id="cite_ref-Sep_2019_183-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-Sep_2019-183"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup> [Block 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Launch_System "Space Launch System") development will most likely start in the late 2020s after NASA is regularly visiting the lunar surface and shifts focus towards Mars.<sup id="cite_ref-slr22May2019_184-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-slr22May2019-184"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup>

In October 2019, NASA authorized [Boeing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boeing "Boeing") to purchase materials in bulk for more SLS rockets ahead of the announcement of a new contract. The contract was expected to support up to ten core stages and eight [Exploration Upper Stages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploration_Upper_Stage "Exploration Upper Stage") for the SLS 1B to transfer heavy payloads of up to 40 metric tons on a lunar trajectory.<sup id="cite_ref-185" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-185"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup>

The SpaceX Starship system is a fully-reusable [super heavy-lift](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Super_heavy-lift_launch_vehicle "Super heavy-lift launch vehicle") Earth-launch system which is under development. It consists of a first-stage booster named [Super-Heavy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Super_Heavy "SpaceX Super Heavy") and a second-stage space vehicle which is generally named [Starship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Starship_(spacecraft) "SpaceX Starship (spacecraft)") and which will have several variants. A Starship HLS mission will use three variants: a tanker, a propellant depot, and the [Starship HLS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Starship_HLS "Starship HLS") itself which will be designed only for lunar landings and takeoffs, and not for Earth landings. Some variants will be able to return to Earth for reuse.

The second-stage Starships are fully self-contained spacecraft, complete with their own propulsion systems. The combined Starship system using standard Starship variants for its second-stage is planned to launch crews and cargo, which may then be used to support the various developmental needs of the Artemis program, and also to support the needs of other NASA and SpaceX programs.

The SpaceX Starship is also qualified to be bid for [Commercial Lunar Payload Services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercial_Lunar_Payload_Services "Commercial Lunar Payload Services") (CLPS) launches, and in 2021 was the winning NASA bid for a crewed lunar landing.<sup id="cite_ref-spacex_hls_186-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-spacex_hls-186"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-187"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-188"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup>

The SpaceX Falcon Heavy is a partially reusable heavy-lift launcher. It will be used to launch the first two Gateway modules into NRHO.<sup id="cite_ref-189" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-189"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> It will also be used to launch the Dragon XL spacecraft on supply missions to Gateway,<sup id="cite_ref-dragonXL_190-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-dragonXL-190"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup> and it is qualified to be bid for other launches under the CLPS program. It was selected under CLPS to launch the VIPER mission, though this mission was later cancelled in 2024 due to cost overruns and mission delays.<sup id="cite_ref-nasa-20240717_191-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-nasa-20240717-191"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup>

Under the CLPS (Commercial Lunar Payload Service) program, qualified CLPS vendors can use any launcher that meets their mission requirements.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/ArtemisI_Orion_EMI_Feb.jpg/260px-ArtemisI_Orion_EMI_Feb.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:ArtemisI_Orion_EMI_Feb.jpg)

NASA's Orion spacecraft undergoing final tests

Orion is a class of [partially reusable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reusable_spacecraft "Reusable spacecraft") spacecraft to be used in the Artemis program. The spacecraft consists of a Crew Module (CM) [space capsule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_capsule "Space capsule") designed by [Lockheed Martin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lockheed_Martin "Lockheed Martin") and the [European Service Module](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Service_Module "European Service Module") (ESM) manufactured by [Airbus Defence and Space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Airbus_Defence_and_Space "Airbus Defence and Space"). Capable of supporting a crew of six beyond [low Earth orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit "Low Earth orbit"), Orion is equipped with [solar panels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_panels_on_spacecraft "Solar panels on spacecraft"), an [automated docking system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA_Docking_System "NASA Docking System"), and [glass cockpit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glass_cockpit "Glass cockpit") interfaces modeled after those used in the [Boeing 787 Dreamliner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boeing_787_Dreamliner "Boeing 787 Dreamliner"). It has a single [AJ10](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AJ10 "AJ10") engine for primary propulsion, and others including [reaction control system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reaction_control_system "Reaction control system") engines. Although designed to be compatible with other [launch vehicles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Launch_vehicle "Launch vehicle"), Orion is primarily intended to launch atop a [Space Launch System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Launch_System "Space Launch System") (SLS) rocket, with a tower [launch escape system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Launch_escape_system "Launch escape system").

Orion was originally conceived by Lockheed Martin as a proposal for the [Crew Exploration Vehicle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crew_Exploration_Vehicle "Crew Exploration Vehicle") (CEV) to be used in NASA's [Constellation program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constellation_program "Constellation program"). Following the cancellation of the Constellation program in 2010, Orion was heavily redesigned for use in NASA's Journey to Mars initiative; later named Moon to Mars. The [SLS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Launch_System "Space Launch System") replaced the [Ares I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ares_I "Ares I") as Orion's primary launch vehicle, and the service module was replaced with a design based on the [European Space Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Space_Agency "European Space Agency")'s [Automated Transfer Vehicle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle "Automated Transfer Vehicle"). A development version of Orion's CM was launched in 2014 during [Exploration Flight Test-1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploration_Flight_Test-1 "Exploration Flight Test-1"), while at least four test articles were produced. By 2022, three flight-worthy Orion crew modules have been built, with an additional one ordered, for use in the Artemis program; the first of these was due to be launched on 30 November 2020, however [Artemis I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_I "Artemis I") did not launch until 16 November 2022.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Gateway_Space_Station_Module_Map.jpg/530px-Gateway_Space_Station_Module_Map.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gateway_Space_Station_Module_Map.jpg)

A conceptual advanced Gateway depicting what Gateway could look like in the late 2020s.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/SpaceX_Dragon_XL_logistics_module_%282%29.jpg/220px-SpaceX_Dragon_XL_logistics_module_%282%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:SpaceX_Dragon_XL_logistics_module_(2).jpg)

Rendering of the proposed SpaceX Dragon XL

On 27 March 2020, SpaceX revealed the Dragon XL resupply spacecraft to carry pressurized and unpressurized cargo, experiments and other supplies to NASA's planned [Lunar Gateway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Gateway "Lunar Gateway") under a [Gateway Logistics Services](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gateway_Logistics_Services "Gateway Logistics Services") (GLS) contract.<sup id="cite_ref-192" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-192"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SpaceX_Dragon_spacenews-3_193-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-SpaceX_Dragon_spacenews-3-193"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup> The equipment delivered by Dragon XL missions could include sample collection materials, spacesuits and other items astronauts may need on the Gateway and on the surface of the [Moon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moon "Moon"), according to [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA"). It will launch on SpaceX [Falcon Heavy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falcon_Heavy "Falcon Heavy") rockets from [LC-39A](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center_Launch_Complex_39A "Kennedy Space Center Launch Complex 39A") at the [Kennedy Space Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center "Kennedy Space Center") in [Florida](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Florida "Florida").<sup id="cite_ref-SpaceX_Dragon_Spaceflight_Now-XL_194-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-SpaceX_Dragon_Spaceflight_Now-XL-194"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup>

The Dragon XL will stay at the Gateway for 6 to 12 months at a time, when research payloads inside and outside the cargo vessel could be operated remotely, even when crews are not present.<sup id="cite_ref-SpaceX_Dragon_Spaceflight_Now-XL_194-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-SpaceX_Dragon_Spaceflight_Now-XL-194"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup> Its payload capacity is expected to be more than 5,000 kilograms (11,000 lb) to lunar orbit.<sup id="cite_ref-195" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-195"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup> There is no requirement for a return to Earth. At the end of the mission the Dragon XL must be able to undock and dispose of the same mass it can bring to the Gateway, by moving the spacecraft to a heliocentric orbit.<sup id="cite_ref-196" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-196"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup>

On 22 February 2023, NASA discussed the Dragon XL's development for the first time since its 2020 unveiling, with Mark Wiese, NASA's manager of deep space logistics for the Gateway program, answering during a panel at [SpaceCom](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Space_Congress&action=edit&redlink=1 "Space Congress (page does not exist)") that NASA has been working with SpaceX to run a series of studies to refine the Dragon XL design and examine cargo configurations and other capabilities that could be enabled by the spacecraft.<sup id="cite_ref-SpaceX_Dragon_SpaceNews-XL_197-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-SpaceX_Dragon_SpaceNews-XL-197"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup> Wiese also elaborated that Dragon XL would be used for initial missions, and stating that "\[NASA\] talked to \[SpaceX\] about [Starship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Starship_(spacecraft) "SpaceX Starship (spacecraft)") evolution and how it all worked together, but we’re not there yet because it's still in a development phase" insinuating that Starship will eventually replace Dragon XL once it completes development.<sup id="cite_ref-SpaceX_Dragon_SpaceNews-XL_197-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-SpaceX_Dragon_SpaceNews-XL-197"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup>

On 29 March 2024, NASA released an article outlining the mission of [Artemis IV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_IV "Artemis IV"), which is to be the first crewed mission to the Lunar Gateway slated for 2028, stating that the Dragon XL will be used to resupply and carry science experiments, however, Artemis IV will take place concurrently with a Starship launch which will dock at the Gateway and help with the assembly of the station.<sup id="cite_ref-198" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-198"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup>

### Human Landing System (HLS)

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=29 "Edit section: Human Landing System (HLS)")\]

The Human Landing System (HLS) is a critical component of the Artemis mission. This system transports crew from lunar orbit (the Gateway or an Orion spacecraft) to the lunar surface, acts as a lunar habitat, and then transports the crew back to lunar orbit. In 2021 SpaceX's [Starship HLS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Starship_HLS "Starship HLS") program was awarded the winning NASA bid for the production of a crewed lunar landing vehicle.<sup id="cite_ref-spacex_hls_186-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-spacex_hls-186"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup> In May 2023, [Blue Origin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_Origin "Blue Origin") was selected as the second provider for lunar lander services.<sup id="cite_ref-199" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-199"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-200"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup>

#### Early developmental history of the HLS vehicle

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=30 "Edit section: Early developmental history of the HLS vehicle")\]

Bidding for NASA's HLS lunar landing vehicle began in 2019. At that time, NASA elected to have the HLS designed and developed by commercial vendors. Eleven competing contracts were initially awarded in May 2019. In April 2020, NASA awarded three competing design contracts, and in April 2021, NASA selected the [Starship HLS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Starship_HLS "Starship HLS") to proceed to development and production.

Separate from its early design and development program for its first HLS spacecraft, NASA retains multiple smaller contracts to study various elements of alternative HLS designs.

The Starship Human Landing System (Starship HLS) was the winner selected by NASA for potential use for long-duration crewed lunar landings as part of NASA's Artemis program.<sup id="cite_ref-ars20210416_47-1" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-ars20210416-47"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASAannouncementApril16_201-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-NASAannouncementApril16-201"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup>

Starship HLS is a variant of SpaceX's [Starship spacecraft](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Starship_(spacecraft) "SpaceX Starship (spacecraft)") optimized to operate on and around the Moon. In contrast to the Starship spacecraft from which it derives, Starship HLS will never re-enter an atmosphere, so it does not have a [heat shield](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat_shield "Heat shield") or [flight control surfaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flight_control_surface "Flight control surface"). In contrast to other proposed HLS designs that used multiple stages, the entire spacecraft will land on the Moon and will then launch from the Moon. Like other Starship variants, Starship HLS has [Raptor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Raptor "SpaceX Raptor") engines mounted at the tail as its primary propulsion system. However, when it is within "tens of meters" of the lunar surface during descent and ascent, it will use high-thrust methane/oxygen RCS thrusters located mid-body instead of the Raptors to avoid raising dust via [plume impingement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plume_impingement "Plume impingement"). A solar array located on the nose below the docking port provides electrical power. Elon Musk stated that Starship HLS would be able to deliver "potentially up to 200 tons" to the lunar surface.

Starship HLS would be launched to Earth orbit using the SpaceX [Super Heavy booster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Starship#Super_Heavy_booster "SpaceX Starship"), and would use a series of tanker spacecraft to refuel the Starship HLS vehicle in Earth orbit for lunar transit and lunar landing operations, a capability referred to as [orbital refueling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_propellant_depot "Orbital propellant depot"). Starship HLS would then boost itself to lunar orbit for rendezvous with Orion. In the mission concept, a NASA Orion spacecraft would carry a NASA crew to the lander, where they would depart and descend to the surface of the Moon. After lunar surface operations, Starship HLS would lift off from the lunar surface acting as a [single-stage-to-orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Single-stage-to-orbit "Single-stage-to-orbit") (SSTO) vehicle and return the crew to Orion.

On 19 May 2023, NASA announced an additional contract to Blue Origin to develop a second crewed lunar lander, which will make its first crewed flight as part of the [Artemis V](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_V "Artemis V") mission. Blue Moon is smaller than the SpaceX HLS lander, having only 20 tons of payload capacity. The lander is fueled with a combination of liquid hydrogen and liquid oxygen propellants.<sup id="cite_ref-BO_HLS_202-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-BO_HLS-202"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup>

NASA's Gateway is an in-development [mini-space station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_station "Space station") in [lunar orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_orbit "Lunar orbit") intended to serve as a solar-powered communication hub, science laboratory, short-term habitation module, and holding area for rovers and other robots.<sup id="cite_ref-Jackson20180911_203-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-Jackson20180911-203"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup> While the project is led by NASA, the Gateway is meant to be developed, serviced, and used in collaboration with commercial and international partners: Canada ([Canadian Space Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadian_Space_Agency "Canadian Space Agency")) (CSA), Europe ([European Space Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Space_Agency "European Space Agency")) (ESA), and Japan ([JAXA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JAXA "JAXA")).

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/2024_Lunar_Gateway_concept_art%2C_March_2020.jpg/310px-2024_Lunar_Gateway_concept_art%2C_March_2020.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:2024_Lunar_Gateway_concept_art,_March_2020.jpg)

Phase 1 early Gateway with Power and Propulsion Element (left), Habitation and Logistics Outpost (center foreground), and cargo spacecraft (center background) depicted

The [Power and Propulsion Element](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Power_and_Propulsion_Element "Power and Propulsion Element") (PPE) started development at the [Jet Propulsion Laboratory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory "Jet Propulsion Laboratory") during the now canceled [Asteroid Redirect Mission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asteroid_Redirect_Mission "Asteroid Redirect Mission") (ARM). The original concept was a robotic, high performance [solar electric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ion_thruster "Ion thruster") spacecraft that would retrieve a multi-ton boulder from an asteroid and bring it to lunar orbit for study.<sup id="cite_ref-204" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-204"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup> When ARM was canceled, the solar electric propulsion was repurposed for the Gateway.<sup id="cite_ref-205" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-205"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-206" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-206"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> The PPE will allow access to the entire lunar surface and act as a [space tug](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_tug "Space tug") for visiting craft.<sup id="cite_ref-207" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-207"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup> It will also serve as the command and communications center of the Gateway.<sup id="cite_ref-208" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-208"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sfn20190524_209-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-sfn20190524-209"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup> The PPE is intended to have a mass of 8–9 tonnes and the capability to generate 50 kW<sup id="cite_ref-3_Nov_210-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-3_Nov-210"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup> of [solar electric power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_electric_power "Solar electric power") for its [ion thrusters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ion_thruster "Ion thruster"), which can be supplemented by chemical propulsion.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight_20170406_211-0" data-immersive-translate-walked="41b7a916-bac5-4728-8b09-09ebec6c3c8b"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-Flight_20170406-211"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup>

The Habitation and Logistics Outpost (HALO),<sup id="cite_ref-HALO_2_212-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-HALO_2-212"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HALO_1_213-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-HALO_1-213"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup> also called the Minimal Habitation Module (MHM) and formerly known as the Utilization Module,<sup id="cite_ref-214"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-214"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup> will be built by [Northrop Grumman Innovation Systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northrop_Grumman_Innovation_Systems "Northrop Grumman Innovation Systems") (NGIS).<sup id="cite_ref-SN_23July2019_215-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-SN_23July2019-215"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MHM_justification_216-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-MHM_justification-216"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup> A single Falcon Heavy equipped with an extended fairing<sup id="cite_ref-nasa_release_20_062_217-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-nasa_release_20_062-217"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup> will launch the PPE together with the HALO in 2027.<sup id="cite_ref-gao-20240731_150-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-gao-20240731-150"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nasa-20210709_151-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-nasa-20210709-151"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup> The HALO is based on a [Cygnus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cygnus_(spacecraft) "Cygnus (spacecraft)") Cargo resupply module<sup id="cite_ref-SN_23July2019_215-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-SN_23July2019-215"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup> to the outside of which radial docking ports, body mounted radiators (BMRs), batteries and communications antennae will be added. The HALO will be a scaled-down habitation module,<sup id="cite_ref-Arc_July2019_218-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-Arc_July2019-218"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup> yet, it will feature a functional pressurized volume providing sufficient command, control, and data handling capabilities, energy storage and power distribution, thermal control, communications and tracking capabilities, two axial and up to two radial docking ports, stowage volume, environmental control and [life support systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Life_support_system "Life support system") to augment the Orion spacecraft and support a crew of four for at least 30 days.<sup id="cite_ref-MHM_justification_216-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-MHM_justification-216"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/NASA_and_ESA%27s_Lunar_Gateway.png/620px-NASA_and_ESA%27s_Lunar_Gateway.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NASA_and_ESA%27s_Lunar_Gateway.png)

Gateway design architecture as of October 2020, which includes European, Japanese, and Russian modules

In late October 2020, NASA and [European Space Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Space_Agency "European Space Agency") (ESA) finalized an agreement to collaborate in the Gateway program. ESA will provide a habitat module in partnership with [JAXA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JAXA "JAXA") (I-HAB) and a refueling module ([ESPRIT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_System_Providing_Refueling,_Infrastructure_and_Telecommunications "European System Providing Refueling, Infrastructure and Telecommunications")). In return, Europe will have three flight opportunities to launch crew aboard the Orion crew capsule, for which they will provide the service module.<sup id="cite_ref-219"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-219"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-220"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup>

In 2024, the HALO module reached substantial completion and entered into the stress test phase, following successful completion of which it will be shipped from Europe to the US in preparation for configuration with the PPE module and launch.<sup id="cite_ref-221"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-221"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup>

On 10 January 2020, [NASA's 22nd astronaut group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA_Astronaut_Group_22 "NASA Astronaut Group 22"), nicknamed the "Turtles", graduated and were assigned to the Artemis program. The group includes two [Canadian Space Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadian_Space_Agency "Canadian Space Agency") (CSA) astronauts. The group earned their nickname from the prior astronaut group, "[The 8-Balls](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA_Astronaut_Group_21 "NASA Astronaut Group 21")", as is a tradition dating back to "[The Mercury Seven](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_Seven "Mercury Seven")" in 1962 which subsequently provided the "[Next Nine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA_Astronaut_Group_2 "NASA Astronaut Group 2")" with their nickname. They were given this name, for the most part, because of [Hurricane Harvey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hurricane_Harvey "Hurricane Harvey"). Some of the astronauts will fly on the Artemis missions to the Moon and may be part of the first crew to fly to Mars.<sup id="cite_ref-222"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-222"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup>

On 9 December 2020, [vice president](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States "Vice President of the United States") [Mike Pence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mike_Pence "Mike Pence") announced the first group of 18 astronauts (all American, including 9 male and 9 female from different backgrounds), the 1st **Artemis team**, who could be selected as astronauts of early missions of the Artemis program:<sup id="cite_ref-223"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-223"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup>

-   [Joe Acaba](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joe_Acaba "Joe Acaba")
-   [Kayla Barron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kayla_Barron "Kayla Barron")
-   [Raja Chari](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raja_Chari "Raja Chari")
-   [Matthew Dominick](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matthew_Dominick "Matthew Dominick")
-   [Victor Glover](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Victor_J._Glover "Victor J. Glover")
-   [Warren Hoburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warren_Hoburg "Warren Hoburg")
-   [Jonny Kim](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jonny_Kim "Jonny Kim")
-   [Christina Koch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christina_Koch "Christina Koch")
-   [Kjell Lindgren](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kjell_Lindgren "Kjell Lindgren")
-   [Nicole Mann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nicole_Mann "Nicole Mann")
-   [Anne McClain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anne_McClain "Anne McClain")
-   [Jessica Meir](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jessica_Meir "Jessica Meir")
-   [Jasmin Moghbeli](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jasmin_Moghbeli "Jasmin Moghbeli")
-   [Kathleen Rubins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kathleen_Rubins "Kathleen Rubins")
-   [Frank Rubio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Francisco_Rubio_(astronaut) "Francisco Rubio (astronaut)")
-   [Scott Tingle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scott_Tingle "Scott Tingle")
-   [Jessica Watkins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jessica_Watkins "Jessica Watkins")
-   [Stephanie Wilson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stephanie_Wilson "Stephanie Wilson")

[Chief Astronaut](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chief_Astronaut "Chief Astronaut") [Reid Wiseman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reid_Wiseman "Reid Wiseman") said in August 2022, however, that all 42 active members of the [NASA Astronaut Corps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA_Astronaut_Corps "NASA Astronaut Corps"), and the ten more training as [NASA Astronaut Group 23](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA_Astronaut_Group_23 "NASA Astronaut Group 23"), are eligible for Artemis II and later flights.<sup id="cite_ref-foust20220822_224-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-foust20220822-224"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup>

## Planned surface operations

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=36 "Edit section: Planned surface operations")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Xemu-eva-Artemis.jpg/310px-Xemu-eva-Artemis.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Xemu-eva-Artemis.jpg)

An artist's rendition of an Artemis astronaut wearing the xEMU spacesuit and xPLS life support backpack during an EVA on the Moon

The Artemis Base Camp will support missions of up to two months and will be used to study technologies to use on future Moon or Mars bases, and then future stationary modules may be used regularly for decades to come through both Government and commercial programs. Most probably it will be a site that has already been visited by prior robotic missions. It will consist of three main modules:

1.  The Surface Habitat (SH) modules, which is the initial dwelling structure and a surface home base for the first residents of the Moon.
2.  The [Lunar Terrain Vehicle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Terrain_Vehicle "Lunar Terrain Vehicle") (LTV), which is an unpressurized rover cart for transporting suited astronauts and cargo around in the vicinity of the Base.
3.  The Pressurized Rovers (PR), a pressurized vehicle complete with small backup habitation facilities, thus enabling multi-day and longer-range explorations tens of kilometers away from the Base.<sup id="cite_ref-artemis_base_camp_225-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-artemis_base_camp-225"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup>

### Transportation on the Moon

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=37 "Edit section: Transportation on the Moon")\]

In 2022, NASA has identified 13 candidate regions near the lunar South Pole for initial landing and inspection missions.<sup id="cite_ref-226"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-226"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup>

#### Ground transportation development

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=39 "Edit section: Ground transportation development")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/NASA_Lunar_Terrain_Vehicle.png/310px-NASA_Lunar_Terrain_Vehicle.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NASA_Lunar_Terrain_Vehicle.png)

NASA's baseline Lunar Terrain Vehicle

In February 2020, NASA released two [requests for information](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Requests_for_information "Requests for information") regarding both a crewed and uncrewed unpressurized surface rover. The latter, Lunar Terrain Vehicle (LTV) would be prepositioned by a CLPS vehicle before the Artemis III mission. It would be used to transport crews around the exploration site and serve a similar function to the Apollo [Lunar Roving Vehicle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Roving_Vehicle "Lunar Roving Vehicle"). In July 2020, NASA established a program office for the rover at the Johnson Space Center in Houston.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_227-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-:2-227"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup>

NASA has specified its need for a Lunar Terrain Vehicle (LTV) that has a cargo capacity of 800 kg, traversal distances of up to 20 km without battery recharging, continuous operations for 8 hours within a 24-hour period, the ability to survive the lunar night, and the ability to traverse grades as steep as ±20 degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-artemis_base_camp_225-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-artemis_base_camp-225"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup>

On 3 April 2024, NASA announced that [Intuitive Machines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intuitive_Machines "Intuitive Machines"), [Lunar Outpost](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Outpost_(company) "Lunar Outpost (company)") and Venturi Astrolab are the three companies developing the LTV in a 12-month feasibility and demo phase.<sup id="cite_ref-228"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-228"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup> A source selection statement by NASA provided further details on cost and overall feasibility on 9 April 2024. The Intuitive Machines proposal was for $1.692 billion, Lunar Outpost for $1.727 billion and Astrolab for $1.928 billion to develop the vehicle.<sup id="cite_ref-229"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-229"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup>

### Shelter building construction

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=40 "Edit section: Shelter building construction")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Artemis_Base_Camp.png/310px-Artemis_Base_Camp.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Artemis_Base_Camp.png)

A render of the Artemis Base Camp

The Artemis Base Camp is the proposed lunar base to be established at the end of the 2020s. The Base camp is to be located in the south pole region near the two adjacent [Shackleton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shackleton_(crater) "Shackleton (crater)") and [de-Gerlache](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De_Gerlache_(crater) "De Gerlache (crater)") craters,<sup id="cite_ref-230"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-230"><span>[</span>225<span>]</span></a></sup> due to this area's wide variety of lunar geography and also due to the abundance of water ice that is believed to exist in the lunar soils of the crater floors. The environs of these craters fall under the guidelines of the [Outer Space Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outer_Space_Treaty "Outer Space Treaty").<sup id="cite_ref-:2_227-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-:2-227"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-231"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-231"><span>[</span>226<span>]</span></a></sup>

#### Foundational Surface Habitat

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=41 "Edit section: Foundational Surface Habitat")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/NASA_Foundation_Surface_Habitat.png/310px-NASA_Foundation_Surface_Habitat.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NASA_Foundation_Surface_Habitat.png)

A render of the Foundational Surface Habitat (stationary configuration)

Most of the information about the Surface Habitat (SH) modules comes from studies and launch manifests which include a reference to its launch. It will be commercially built and commercially launched in the early 2030s along with the [Pressurized Vehicle (PV)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Exploration_Vehicle "Space Exploration Vehicle").<sup id="cite_ref-:1_232-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-:1-232"><span>[</span>227<span>]</span></a></sup> The SH was formerly referred to as the Artemis Surface Asset. Current launch plans show that landing it on the surface would be similar to the HLS. The SH would be sent to the Gateway where it would then be attached to a descent stage and subsequently transported to the lunar surface with a commercial launcher and lander. It would use the same lunar transfer stage as used for the HLS. Other designs from 2019 see it being launched from an [SLS Block 1B](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Launch_System "Space Launch System") as a single unit and landing directly on the surface. It would then be hooked up to a surface power system launched by a CLPS mission and tested by the Artemis VI crew.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_227-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-:2-227"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ars-lunar-outpost_233-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-ars-lunar-outpost-233"><span>[</span>228<span>]</span></a></sup> The Italian Space Agency signed a contract with Thales Alenia Space in late 2023 for the Multi Purpose Habitat, which may become the second module for the Artemis Base Camp.<sup id="cite_ref-234"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-234"><span>[</span>229<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-235"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-235"><span>[</span>230<span>]</span></a></sup>

### Resource prospecting and research programs

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=42 "Edit section: Resource prospecting and research programs")\]

As of February 2020, a lunar stay during a Phase 1 Artemis mission will be about seven days and will have five [extravehicular activities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extravehicular_activities "Extravehicular activities") (EVA). A _notional_ concept of operations, i.e., a hypothetical but possible plan, would include the following: On Day 1 of the stay, astronauts touchdown on the Moon but do not conduct an EVA. Instead, they prepare for the EVA scheduled for the next day, in what is referred to as "The Road to EVA".<sup id="cite_ref-ntrs.nasa.gov_236-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-ntrs.nasa.gov-236"><span>[</span>231<span>]</span></a></sup>

On Day 2, the astronauts open the hatch on the Human Landing System and embark on EVA 1, which will be six hours long. It will include collecting a contingency sample, conducting public affairs activities, deploying the experiment package, and acquiring samples. The astronauts will stay close to the landing site on this first EVA. EVA 2 begins on day 3. The astronauts characterize and collect samples from [permanently shadowed regions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permanently_shadowed_crater "Permanently shadowed crater"). Unlike the previous EVA, the astronauts will go farther from the landing site, up to 2 kilometres (1.2 mi), and up and down slopes of 20°.<sup id="cite_ref-ntrs.nasa.gov_236-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-ntrs.nasa.gov-236"><span>[</span>231<span>]</span></a></sup>

Day 4 will not include an EVA, but Day 5 will. EVA 3 may include activities such as collecting samples from an ejecta blanket. Day 6 will have the two astronauts deploy a geotechnical instrument alongside an environmental monitoring station for [in-situ resource utilization (ISRU)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/In_situ_resource_utilization "In situ resource utilization"). Day 7 will have the final and shortest EVA. This EVA will last one hour, rather than the others' six hours in duration from egress to ingress, and mostly comprises preparations for the lunar ascent, including jettisoning hardware. Once the final EVA is concluded, the astronauts will return to the Human Landing System and the vehicle will launch from the surface and join up with Orion/Gateway.<sup id="cite_ref-ntrs.nasa.gov_236-2"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-ntrs.nasa.gov-236"><span>[</span>231<span>]</span></a></sup>

#### Pressurized rover ("Mobile Habitat")

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=43 "Edit section: Pressurized rover ("Mobile Habitat")")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/NASA_Habitable_Mobility_Platform.png/310px-NASA_Habitable_Mobility_Platform.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NASA_Habitable_Mobility_Platform.png)

NASA Habitable Mobility Platform based on the past Constellation [Space Exploration Vehicle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Exploration_Vehicle "Space Exploration Vehicle")

The Pressurized Rover (PR) is a large, pressurized module used to enable crewed operation across large distances and live for multiple days. NASA had developed multiple pressurized rovers including what was formerly called the [Space Exploration Vehicle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Exploration_Vehicle "Space Exploration Vehicle") (SEV). This rover was built for the [Constellation program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constellation_program "Constellation program") and was fabricated and then tested. In the 2020 flight manifest it was later referred to as the "Mobile Habitat" suggesting it could fill a similar role to the [ILREC Lunar Bus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Lunar_Resources_Exploration_Concept "International Lunar Resources Exploration Concept"). It would be ready for the crew to use on the surface but could also be autonomously controlled from the Gateway or other locations.

Mark Kirasich, who is the acting director of NASA's Advanced Exploration Systems, has stated that the current plan is to partner with [JAXA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JAXA "JAXA") and [Toyota](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toyota "Toyota") to develop a closed cabin rover to support crews for up to 14 days (currently known as [Lunar Cruiser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lunar_Cruiser "Lunar Cruiser")). "It's very important to our leadership at the moment to involve JAXA in a major surface element", he said. "... The Japanese, and their auto industry, have a very strong interest in rover-type things. So, there was an idea to—even though we have done a lot of work—to let the Japanese lead development of a pressurized rover. So right now, that's the direction we're heading in".

In regard to the PR, senior-lunar-scientist Clive Neal said "Under Constellation, NASA had a sophisticated rover put together. It's pretty sad if it's never going to get to the Moon". However Neal also said that he understands the different mission objectives between the Constellation Program and those of the Artemis Program, and the need of the Artemis Program to focus more on international collaboration.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_227-3"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-:2-227"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-237"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-237"><span>[</span>232<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-238"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-238"><span>[</span>233<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-239"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-239"><span>[</span>234<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-240"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-240"><span>[</span>235<span>]</span></a></sup>

On 9 April 2024, it was announced that JAXA and NASA had signed an agreement stipulating that Japan would join the pressurized rover collaboration venture and would design, develop, and operate a rover for crewed and uncrewed exploration of the Moon. In return, NASA will provide the launch and delivery of the rover to the Moon, as well as providing seats for two Japanese astronaut missions to the lunar surface, with the goal of these astronauts being the first non-Americans to travel to the Moon's surface. The pressurized rover is planned to accommodate two astronauts for up to 30 days outside. NASA plans to use the pressurized rover from Artemis VII and subsequent missions, over an approximate 10 year lifespan.<sup id="cite_ref-241"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-241"><span>[</span>236<span>]</span></a></sup>

## Specialized lunar equipment development

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=44 "Edit section: Specialized lunar equipment development")\]

### Artemis space suits

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=45 "Edit section: Artemis space suits")\]

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Artemis_HLS_xEMU_NASA.jpg/220px-Artemis_HLS_xEMU_NASA.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Artemis_HLS_xEMU_NASA.jpg)

xEMU suit for lunar surface [extravehicular activity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extravehicular_activity "Extravehicular activity") (EVA)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Artemis_Orion_OCSS_Suit_NASA.jpg/170px-Artemis_Orion_OCSS_Suit_NASA.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Artemis_Orion_OCSS_Suit_NASA.jpg)

OCSS suit for launch and reentry

The Artemis program will make use of two types of [space suit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_suit "Space suit") revealed in October 2019: the [Exploration Extravehicular Mobility Unit (xEMU)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extravehicular_Mobility_Unit#Future_use_and_replacement "Extravehicular Mobility Unit"),<sup id="cite_ref-xEMU_242-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-xEMU-242"><span>[</span>237<span>]</span></a></sup> and the Orion Crew Survival System (OCSS).<sup id="cite_ref-OCSS_243-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-OCSS-243"><span>[</span>238<span>]</span></a></sup>

On 10 August 2021, a [NASA Office of Inspector General](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA_Office_of_Inspector_General "NASA Office of Inspector General") audit reported a conclusion that the spacesuits would not be ready until April 2025 at the earliest, likely delaying the mission from the then planned late 2024.<sup id="cite_ref-suitdelay_244-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-suitdelay-244"><span>[</span>239<span>]</span></a></sup> In response to the IG report, [SpaceX](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX "SpaceX") indicated that they could provide the suits.<sup id="cite_ref-245"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-245"><span>[</span>240<span>]</span></a></sup>

#### Commercial spacesuits

\[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Artemis_program&action=edit&section=46 "Edit section: Commercial spacesuits")\]

NASA published a draft RFP to procure commercially-produced spacesuits in order to meet the 2024 schedule.<sup id="cite_ref-246"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-246"><span>[</span>241<span>]</span></a></sup> On 2 June 2022, NASA announced that commercially produced spacesuits would be developed by [Axiom Space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axiom_Space "Axiom Space") and [Collins Aerospace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Collins_Aerospace "Collins Aerospace").<sup id="cite_ref-247"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-247"><span>[</span>242<span>]</span></a></sup> In early 2024, the development reached the critical design and test phase.<sup id="cite_ref-248"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-248"><span>[</span>243<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-249"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-249"><span>[</span>244<span>]</span></a></sup>

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/EFT-1_Orion_recovery.5.jpg/220px-EFT-1_Orion_recovery.5.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:EFT-1_Orion_recovery.5.jpg)

The Orion capsule in the [Pacific Ocean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific_Ocean "Pacific Ocean"), following the [Exploration Flight Test-1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploration_Flight_Test-1 "Exploration Flight Test-1") mission

A prototype version of the Orion Crew Module was launched on [Exploration Flight Test-1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploration_Flight_Test-1 "Exploration Flight Test-1") on 5 December 2014<sup id="cite_ref-eft1-1_250-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-eft1-1-250"><span>[</span>245<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-eft1-2_251-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-eft1-2-251"><span>[</span>246<span>]</span></a></sup> atop a [Delta IV Heavy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Delta_IV_Heavy "Delta IV Heavy") rocket. Its [reaction control system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reaction_control_system "Reaction control system") and other components were tested during two [medium Earth orbits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medium_Earth_Orbit "Medium Earth Orbit"), reaching an apogee of 5,800 km (3,600 mi) and crossing the [Van Allen radiation belts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Van_Allen_Belts "Van Allen Belts") before making a high-energy re-entry at 32,000 km/h (20,000 mph).<sup id="cite_ref-eft1-3_252-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-eft1-3-252"><span>[</span>247<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-eft1-4_253-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-eft1-4-253"><span>[</span>248<span>]</span></a></sup>

The [Ascent Abort-2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ascent_Abort-2 "Ascent Abort-2") test on 2 July 2019 tested the final iteration of the launch abort system on a 10,000 kg (22,000 lb) Orion [boilerplate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boilerplate_(spaceflight) "Boilerplate (spaceflight)") at maximum aerodynamic load,<sup id="cite_ref-aa2-1_254-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-aa2-1-254"><span>[</span>249<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aa2-2_255-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-aa2-2-255"><span>[</span>250<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aa2-3_256-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-aa2-3-256"><span>[</span>251<span>]</span></a></sup> using a custom [Minotaur IV](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minotaur_IV "Minotaur IV")\-derived launch vehicle built by [Orbital ATK](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_ATK "Orbital ATK").<sup id="cite_ref-aa2-3_256-1"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-aa2-3-256"><span>[</span>251<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aa2-4_257-0"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artemis_program#cite_note-aa2-4-257"><span>[</span>252<span>]</span></a></sup>

As of November 2022, all crewed Artemis missions will launch on the Space Launch System from [Kennedy Space Center Launch Complex 39B](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center_Launch_Complex_39B "Kennedy Space Center Launch Complex 39B"). Current plans call for some supporting hardware to be launched on other vehicles and from other launch pads.
